<div id="review_954752492" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/954752492" />
    <a title="Alice Poon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28700577-alice-poon"><img alt="Alice Poon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499099841p2/28700577.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/954752492?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alice Poon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alice Poon" href="/user/show/28700577-alice-poon">Alice Poon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28700577-alice-poon?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer954752492" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18063320302680811819"><br/>This is a perspicacious study of characters whose fates are indubitably shaped by their respective aspirations, natural tendencies and outlook on life. Maugham doubles as the narrator and as one of the cast (a writer), and through his narration, readers are engaged with the intertwining stories of the various characters, who move between post-WWI Europe, America and India.<br/><br/>Isabel and Larry, two young American lovers who have known each other from childhood, discover the unbridgeable gulf between </span>
  <span id="freeText18063320302680811819" style="display:none"><br />This is a perspicacious study of characters whose fates are indubitably shaped by their respective aspirations, natural tendencies and outlook on life. Maugham doubles as the narrator and as one of the cast (a writer), and through his narration, readers are engaged with the intertwining stories of the various characters, who move between post-WWI Europe, America and India.<br /><br />Isabel and Larry, two young American lovers who have known each other from childhood, discover the unbridgeable gulf between them before it’s too late, and break off their engagement. With her eyes set upon having an easy and well-provided-for life, Isabel marries a local wealthy heir, Gray, who adores her, but in her heart she can’t let go of penniless Larry. Larry, having witnessed his best friend killed in war trying to save him, goes off on a prolonged knapsack journey to Europe and then to India in quest for the true meaning of life. He has no regrets about breaking off with Isabel and gets on with living life his own way, aiming at cultivating his intellect and at self-perfection. When he decides to marry a childhood friend in an attempt to save her from a downward spiral, Isabel takes it upon herself to sabotage the intended union. Only when confronted by Maugham much later is she forced to confess her malice in the act.<br /><br />Isabel’s uncle Elliott is a narcissistic socialite who hobnobs with the European aristocracy but is kindly disposed towards his sister and niece. He often dispenses meddling advice to people he is fond of, thinking he is doing them a great favor. In his eyes, Larry is all wrong for Isabel, because he is against making something out of himself. He thinks Gray is the obvious choice as a spouse for Isabel. Later, when Gray and Isabel are ruined by the 1929 stock market crash, Elliott lends them a generous helping hand. But at his senile age, even in his sickbed, Elliott still gets upset at not having been invited to a glamorous party.<br /><br />I love how Maugham imparts his own wisdom about the philosophy of living through Larry in this richly drawn novel. This is a passage I like:<br /><br /><i>Nothing in the world is permanent, and we’re foolish when we ask anything to last, but surely we’re still more foolish not to take delight in it while we have it. If change is of the essence of existence, one would have thought it only sensible to make it the premise of our philosophy.</i><br /><br />Overall, it is a realistic and thought-provoking story masterfully told, and is the best by Maugham I’ve read so far. I’m giving it 5 deserving stars!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18063320302680811819" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2577946283" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2577946283" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2577946283?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2577946283" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6453234497097030582">Chatty, erudite, engrossing and thoroughly entertaining.<br/><br/>W. Somerset Maugham’s 1944 publication, called by many his most ambitious work, centers around a group of friends from Chicago whose lives are chronicled by the narrator over a period of more than twenty years from before the first World War, though the Great Depression and after World War II.<br/><br/>The most stimulating character is Larry Darrell, whose journey towards enlightenment is almost Hessian in its eloquence and single-mindedness. Maugham</span>
  <span id="freeText6453234497097030582" style="display:none">Chatty, erudite, engrossing and thoroughly entertaining.<br><br>W. Somerset Maugham’s 1944 publication, called by many his most ambitious work, centers around a group of friends from Chicago whose lives are chronicled by the narrator over a period of more than twenty years from before the first World War, though the Great Depression and after World War II.<br><br>The most stimulating character is Larry Darrell, whose journey towards enlightenment is almost Hessian in its eloquence and single-mindedness. Maugham draws Darrell as an esoteric seeker after perfection who walks away from the material world and chases his own agenda, in stark contrast to the more worldly pursuits of his friends. Darrell’s pre-marriage conversation with Isabel about what is best in life is rare literary magic. A more conventional, but less talented writer, would have used Larry’s quest as a vehicle to explore the spiritual over the material, but Maugham is too much of a well-rounded man of the world and instead blends Larry’s story with a more universal tale of successes and failures.<br><br>** Just a thought – was the 1980s TV show Newhart’s characters of Larry, Darrell and his other brother Darrell a playful homage?<br><br>This book is full of brilliant characterizations and the dialogue was absorbing. This read like a garrulous visit with friends over years; gossipy and fun but also revealing Maugham’s astute judge of character and situation. The reader finds hidden treasures of sophisticated observation and social commentary delivered with a knowing understanding of the human condition.<br><br>This is also a very American novel, even though from the perspective of an Englishman and much of the action is in Europe and abroad. Maugham’s theme of attainment, love and loss transcends a pedestrian account of haves and haves nots and is simply a very good story.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1468962201i/19764843._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="281" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="6453234497097030582" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1223409830" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1223409830" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1223409830?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1223409830" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1146742310501587284">Oh, Mr. Maugham, there are moments when I love you so much I could burst. Moments when I wish there were a six star rating, so I could put it into your hands and say "I got that part and it resonated with me." Moments when I want to say, "enough of that, get back to the story", only to find <i>That</i> is the story, <i>That</i> is the heart.<br/><br/>This novel made me wish to live in the post WWI twenties and have endless possibilities open to me. It made me examine the life I have lived and wonder if I couldn't have </span>
  <span id="freeText1146742310501587284" style="display:none">Oh, Mr. Maugham, there are moments when I love you so much I could burst. Moments when I wish there were a six star rating, so I could put it into your hands and say "I got that part and it resonated with me." Moments when I want to say, "enough of that, get back to the story", only to find <i>That</i> is the story, <i>That</i> is the heart.<br /><br />This novel made me wish to live in the post WWI twenties and have endless possibilities open to me. It made me examine the life I have lived and wonder if I couldn't have gotten more out of it if I had been bolder or less worried.<br /> "It's strange how many people suffer from it (fear). I don't mean fear of closed spaces and fear of heights, but fear of death and, what's worse, fear of life. Often they're people who seem in the best of health, prosperous, without any worry, and yet they're tortured by it. I've sometimes thought it was the most besetting humour of men, and I asked myself at one time if it was due to some deep animal instinct that man has inherited from that primeval something that first felt the thrill of life."<br />How any things have I not done in life because I was afraid to try them? More than a few I can remember. Here at the end, I wish I had been braver, bolder and, yes, a little crazier.<br /><br />This is surely amongst the best, if not the best, of Maugham's works I have found so far. By inserting himself into the novel, he makes it seem so vital and real, and even while understanding it as a contrivance, it lends these characters heft and weight and importance. For each of them, life is about choices and one has to question which of these characters is the most fulfilled. In the end we are told they all get what they wanted, but did they? Eliot wants to be very important, but is he? Does anyone truly want death? Should we choose security over adventure and love? Is a higher truth worth striving for? Can a man ever identify and know God?<br /><br />The Maugham who is a character in this book is only an observer, no wiser than the others, unable to give us the answers and willing to accept the failures. The Maugham who wrote this book is wise and savvy and enlightened. He knows. This book is like an onion. I kept peeling it back to find another layer, and another layer, and a layer deeper even than that.<br /><br />"Unless love is passion, it's not love, but something else, and passion thrives not on satisfaction, but on impediment. What d'you suppose Keats meant when he told the lover on his Grecian urn not to grieve? 'Forever wilt thou love, and she be fair!' Why? Because she was unattainable, and however madly the lover pursued she still eluded him."<br /><br />Perhaps true knowledge is as unattainable as love, always more to know, always another place to seek in, always a little out of our reach. In the hands of God, who might reveal it to us at the moment of our deaths or might send us back to strive again and again until we have gotten it right.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1146742310501587284" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1161796573" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1161796573" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1161796573?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Chrissie by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/250817-inder">Inder</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=love">love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=philo-psychol">philo-psychol</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2018-read">2018-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=spain">spain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1161796573" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8204779213963520755"><u>The way Maugham crafts this is to be applauded; it reads in just the way it should</u>. <br/><br/>Only a sentence or two in, I was instantly compelled to read more. Maugham is a character in the story, he even retains his own name. That he is an author, that he studied medicine, that he has travelled is spoken of and is clearly evident by the very details woven into the tale. He speaks of how this is a novel, but then we are told it isn’t. It is about a person he knew. He tells us that what he doesn’t know ab</span>
  <span id="freeText8204779213963520755" style="display:none"><u>The way Maugham crafts this is to be applauded; it reads in just the way it should</u>. <br /><br />Only a sentence or two in, I was instantly compelled to read more. Maugham is a character in the story, he even retains his own name. That he is an author, that he studied medicine, that he has travelled is spoken of and is clearly evident by the very details woven into the tale. He speaks of how this is a novel, but then we are told it isn’t. It is about a person he knew. He tells us that what he doesn’t know about the characters’ lives will just be left out. Names will be changed and dialogs invented to make it into a well flowing tale. I like how he inserts himself into the story. Immediately one’s interest is piqued. Who could Maugham be writing about? Curious? Check out Wiki but after completing the book. There is no definitive answer, but several possibilities are discussed. <br /><br />This is a book about several Americans and about their lives abroad, in France, England, Germany, Italy, India and Spain. It is set between the wars. Maugham insists, and I do agree, that where you are born shapes you and leaves an indelible stamp upon your character. The countries you come to reside in will shape you too. Stated at the very beginning, this is another example of how Maugham teases you with tidbits, pulling you into the story. <br /><br />Maugham’s exceptional ability to tell the story in just the right way is in full force up to the end. At the beginning of part VI, of a total of VII, we are told that if you want you can skip it; it is only a conversation between Maugham and Larry Darell. At this point it is clear that Larry is one of the central if not the most important of the book’s protagonists and that here will be explained why Maugham wrote the book! One grabs the book, reads on, skipping nothing! Having finished the section one realizes that it does lie apart. It focuses on transcendentalism, core Hindu beliefs and Larry’s search for God, faith and happiness. He has encountered yogis and has resided in an ashrama in India. Reading the sectionr <b>is</b> essential, but its religious content did not speak to me. Regardless, my point is that Maugham cleverly piques the reader’s interest while at the time separating the religious content from the rest. Quite simply, this is very cleverly done!<br /><br /><u>Maugham has a knack for drawing characters</u>. Character portrayal is an important part of the book. Each is astutely depicted in manner, in behavior and in appearance--the shape of hands, the curve of a lip, the depth of one’s eye sockets, their clothes and how the clothes hang on their bodies. What each character says fits perfectly who they are, their appearance, their body movements and the very essence of their way of being. The characters become separate individuals with clear identities. None are drawn in black and white but are instead a realistic mix of good and bad qualities. Though each is meticulously drawn, I remained separate from them. I observed them and they felt genuine to me, but except for one I did not come to care for them. Elliot Templeton is that one exception. He is a snob. His goal in life is to excel in the rules of society, to reach the top echelon of class. I should despise him, but I don’t because he is also generous and kind and he would never even contemplate letting family down. Talk about characters with marvelous contradictions! Maugham does not let Elliot down either, but this is a part of the story I will not divulge other than to say that the episode will surely make you smile.<br /><br />I would like to tell you more about each character, but doing so would decrease one’s enjoyment of the book. Observing their differences is revealing. They so contrast with each other. Happiness for one is the opposite for another, which is what I think Maugham is at least partially trying to say with the book.<br /><br />In that the story’s narrator, Maugham, jumps over years of which he knows nothing, there are holes in the string of events. For this reason, <u>the story reads as connected stories, interlinked by the central characters</u>. You do not get pulled in as you might otherwise.<br /><br />A person may read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31196.The_Razor_s_Edge" title="The Razor's Edge by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">The Razor's Edge</a> for its philosophical and religious content, for its themes focusing upon snobbism, pursuit of happiness and some individuals’ self-destructive tendencies, but <u>what I enjoyed most was how Maugham’s knowledge of art, poetry, literature and European culture, traditions, sites and places, particularly those in France, are woven into the story</u>.<br /><br />The audiobook I listened to was narrated by Gordon Griffin. I have given the narration four stars. It is easy to follow and is read at a good speed. Although I didn’t love the intonation used for Elliot, I can understand why he chose to use the intonation he does. The women characters are not done badly either. When sweet, naïve, flirtatious Isabel Bradley, who is obsessed with Larry, explodes into anger you certainly hear it. This is very well done<br /><br />Another good thing about Maugham’s books is that each offers you a completely different story. Each is different in style, in content and in how the story is told.<br /><br />My ratings of Maugham’s books:<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3616323.Mrs_Craddock" title="Mrs Craddock by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Mrs Craddock</a> 4 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/191793.Cakes_and_Ale" title="Cakes and Ale by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Cakes and Ale</a> 4 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99664.The_Painted_Veil" title="The Painted Veil by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">The Painted Veil</a> 4 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31196.The_Razor_s_Edge" title="The Razor's Edge by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">The Razor's Edge</a> 3 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/397539.Christmas_Holiday" title="Christmas Holiday by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Christmas Holiday</a> 3 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/44796.The_Moon_and_Sixpence" title="The Moon and Sixpence by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">The Moon and Sixpence</a> 2 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31548.Of_Human_Bondage" title="Of Human Bondage by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Of Human Bondage</a> 2 stars<br /><br />There are fortunately many more left for me to read. The guy was certainly prolific!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8204779213963520755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2453982" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2453982" />
    <a title="Wealhtheow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76129-wealhtheow"><img alt="Wealhtheow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1178337774p2/76129.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2453982?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wealhtheow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wealhtheow" href="/user/show/76129-wealhtheow">Wealhtheow</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140185232" href="/book/show/117834.The_Razor_s_Edge">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76129-wealhtheow?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2453982" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7040389100385177032">When I first read this, I was too overwhelmed to try to sum up my feelings about it with just a bit of text. (Plus, I wasn't on Goodreads at the time.) There is still no earthly way that I can convey how fantastic this book is, but I did want to mention what still strikes me years later. <br/><br/>In the hands of most authors, this book would focus on Larry, the young man who abandons a life of privilege to seek enlightenment and meaning after terrible experiences in the Great War. His fiancee, who breaks</span>
  <span id="freeText7040389100385177032" style="display:none">When I first read this, I was too overwhelmed to try to sum up my feelings about it with just a bit of text. (Plus, I wasn't on Goodreads at the time.) There is still no earthly way that I can convey how fantastic this book is, but I did want to mention what still strikes me years later. <br /><br />In the hands of most authors, this book would focus on Larry, the young man who abandons a life of privilege to seek enlightenment and meaning after terrible experiences in the Great War. His fiancee, who breaks her engagement with him when she realizes that his priorities will never be worldly, would be the villain, an example of how foolish and narrow-minded Society can be. And Maugham follows this cliche--to a point. He shows us all the downsides of bowing to the conservative demands of other people's opinions. But he also gives the fiancee a voice. And she, in a truly unique flip on the "support the Great Thinker!" trope, asks, (I'm paraphrasing here--Maugham's words are more natural and nuanced) "and what are we to live on? If he's out chasing transcendentalism, how am I supposed to feed myself, or any children we have? I love him, but I have my own set of values." And even better, Isabel refuses to feel bad about this choice. She likes pearls and nice food, and doesn't want to travel, or live in a hovel. She's not interested in whether Larry understands his place in the universe or not. <br /><br />I have read countless stories in which the characters who are not spiritual or artistic are not valued. They are simply the villains or the foils. Being "worldly" is always a condemnation. Pursuing money in any way is seen as crass and loathsome. And as for not marrying for love! Isabel is not the heroine of this book; she has an ordinary intellect and a conventional, unexciting life. But I don't think Larry was the hero, either. Maugham does not present one choice as completely right, and the other as completely wrong. To be perfectly frank, I can't think of another male author (of Maugham's era or before) who understood that not everyone can live on sensibility; that supporting an artist or a saint is all and very well, but there's more to life than being someone's adoring crutch. I am amazed at his sensitivity. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7040389100385177032" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_13472962" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13472962" />
    <a title="Ben" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/250757-ben"><img alt="Ben" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1186160070p2/250757.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13472962?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/250757-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/250757-ben?shelf=chicago-books-authors">chicago-books-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/250757-ben?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13472962" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15877109122803459414">So let me start with a few of the reasons why I shouldn't have liked this book.<br/>-I usually prefer contemporary fiction.<br/>-The Americans are, for the most part, sad, sad characters. Eliot is obsessed with society and culture to the detriment of love and emotion. Isabel wouldn't marry Larry because he would never be rich, and she was disappointed when she inherited some Picassos and Matisses because they wouldn't match her modern decor. Gray was somewhat single minded about work.<br/>-The level of detail </span>
  <span id="freeText15877109122803459414" style="display:none">So let me start with a few of the reasons why I shouldn't have liked this book.<br />-I usually prefer contemporary fiction.<br />-The Americans are, for the most part, sad, sad characters. Eliot is obsessed with society and culture to the detriment of love and emotion. Isabel wouldn't marry Larry because he would never be rich, and she was disappointed when she inherited some Picassos and Matisses because they wouldn't match her modern decor. Gray was somewhat single minded about work.<br />-The level of detail used to describe the three main cities, Chicago, Paris, and London, went a long way to prove that Maugham spent very little time in Chicago, which is, of course, his loss.<br />-Two people told me how good it was. Even though I highly respect the literary opinions of both, (both are over-qualified booksellers, both read as much as I do, and neither has uttered the sentence, 'the da vinci code was the most greatest book I have ever read') whenever someone recommends a book to me it's usually a precursor to that book being crap. I guess I have strange tastes and I generally don't like people telling me what to read.<br />Now that I've gotten through all that I can say, as you've probably guess by my 5 star rating, that I really enjoyed this book. Larry was an incredible character, who's life I was, for the most part, quite jealous of. Maugham disected love, relationships, and emotions. He dealt with tremendous ideas of religion and good/evil. And he did so in a believable way which is rare when an author deals with such immense, important themes. It was kind of a cheat to include himself as a character. He came off as extrememly sensitive to some of the smallest character flaws, but when you create the characters yourself, it makes it easier. All in all, this was a tremendous read which I would recommend to just about everyone, unless of course your favorite book is the da vince code.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15877109122803459414" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140185232" href="/book/show/117834.The_Razor_s_Edge">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3917029-stacia?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3917029-stacia?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3917029-stacia?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1209077004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16618960369735915485">What a keen observer of people Maugham is! This book is just a wonderful delight, amusing & serious at the same time, filled with Maugham's warm, wonderful observations of people. <br/><br/>It's like visiting a bunch of old friends various times throughout the years, telling stories, catching up on gossip, pondering the meaning of life, etc.... Maugham writes beautifully. All the characters are great -- true people with many virtues, but flaws too; the flaws don't detract, though, just make them real. (El</span>
  <span id="freeText16618960369735915485" style="display:none">What a keen observer of people Maugham is! This book is just a wonderful delight, amusing &amp; serious at the same time, filled with Maugham's warm, wonderful observations of people. <br /><br />It's like visiting a bunch of old friends various times throughout the years, telling stories, catching up on gossip, pondering the meaning of life, etc.... Maugham writes beautifully. All the characters are great -- true people with many virtues, but flaws too; the flaws don't detract, though, just make them real. (Elliott becomes the center of attention in every scene he graces! Loved him, lol!) <br /><br />As the book closes, Maugham writes,<br /><blockquote>"But as I was finishing this book, uneasily conscious that I must leave my reader in the air and seeing no way to avoid it, I looked back with my mind's eye on my long narrative to see if there was any way in which I could devise a more satisfactory ending; and to my intense surprise it dawned upon me that without the least intending to I had written nothing more or less than a success story."</blockquote><br />Unlike so many books with "big" events or sad or shocking events used as literary devices, this one is similar to life with a warmth &amp; love of humanity that infuses the characters with more good than bad, events with overall hope, and a pleasant ending. (That doesn't mean there aren't sad events, but those are seen as a part of a normal life where family &amp; friends help one get through &amp; still enjoy life.) By my reckoning, The Razor's Edge is a modern classic &amp; a 5-star book.<br />------------------------<br />Addendum 2017: Just started this book again. What a treat to be meeting all the characters again!</span>

<div id="review_79288223" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/79288223" />
    <a title="·Karen·" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1652898-karen"><img alt="·Karen·" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434742215p2/1652898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/79288223?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="·Karen·" class="user" itemprop="url" name="·Karen·" href="/user/show/1652898-karen">·Karen·</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=f2f-book-group-reads">f2f-book-group-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer79288223" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1409577159233285947">Maugham appears as the narrator in this story of Larry Darrel, a young American who lied about his age to be able to become an airman in the first World War. After a traumatic experience there he no longer wishes to satisfy the expectations of his friends and join his schoolmate Gray's father's brokerage company, or indeed any other reasonable form of earning a living. He wishes to 'loaf'. Isabel, his shallow, mercenary fiancee gives him two years in Paris to get this strange idea out of his sys</span>
  <span id="freeText1409577159233285947" style="display:none">Maugham appears as the narrator in this story of Larry Darrel, a young American who lied about his age to be able to become an airman in the first World War. After a traumatic experience there he no longer wishes to satisfy the expectations of his friends and join his schoolmate Gray's father's brokerage company, or indeed any other reasonable form of earning a living. He wishes to 'loaf'. Isabel, his shallow, mercenary fiancee gives him two years in Paris to get this strange idea out of his system: he embarks on a course of reading, and when Isabel comes to bring him home, he proposes that she should join him on his journey of self-discovery: but she likes the lifestyle that she has always enjoyed and finds his little room nothing but sordid. They part.<br /><br />Over the next ten years we follow Maugham's occasional meetings with Larry, his relations with Isabel and Gray, his friendship with Isabel's snobbish uncle, and a Frenchwoman Suzanne who is able to fill him in on missing parts of Larry's story, a man in search of the spiritual.<br /><br />The whole thing doesn't seem to hang together very well: the structure is episodic, the characters are more embodiments of ideas than real people, the device of various characters unburdening their souls to Maugham is artificial, and yet and yet.... Maugham is masterful as a storyteller and I enjoyed it in spite of its faults. Maugham is so aloof, but that's is what makes his charm: at the end he brings it all together in a wonderfully laid back and tolerant acceptance of each to his own way of finding Nirvana.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1409577159233285947" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_16817832" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16817832" />
    <a title="Trevor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/175635-trevor"><img alt="Trevor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565151545p2/175635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16817832?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trevor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trevor" href="/user/show/175635-trevor">Trevor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/175635-trevor?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16817832" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1862437818475288170">I really enjoy Maugham’s writing. No one writes a snob quite like Maugham does. He generally creates interesting characters – characters that stay with me. Now, I’m hopeless with names and I couldn’t be bothered looking them up, but that woman he falls in love with in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31548.Of_Human_Bondage' title='Of Human Bondage by W. Somerset Maugham' rel='nofollow noopener'>Of Human Bondage</a> and the other woman who is lucky to get away from him in the same book are remarkable characters. Characters that leap off the page in their own way.<br/><br/>This book is supposed to be based on real life – in fact, it clai</span>
  <span id="freeText1862437818475288170" style="display:none">I really enjoy Maugham’s writing. No one writes a snob quite like Maugham does. He generally creates interesting characters – characters that stay with me. Now, I’m hopeless with names and I couldn’t be bothered looking them up, but that woman he falls in love with in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31548.Of_Human_Bondage" title="Of Human Bondage by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Of Human Bondage</a> and the other woman who is lucky to get away from him in the same book are remarkable characters. Characters that leap off the page in their own way.<br /><br />This book is supposed to be based on real life – in fact, it claims to be fact that has been fictionalised to protect the innocent. And there are long bits of this that I really liked, in fact, most of the book I really liked, but if anything the people in this book were more ‘characters’ (I mean, seemed more made up) than the characters in many of his stories. <br /><br />The main point of this one, I guess, is the story of a young man who goes off to war and who has his life saved by a friend who subsequently dies due to rescuing him. This (and possibly other horrors witnessed along the way) makes him question the point of life. People expected him to come back a bit stuffed up – but they also expected him to get better and then to get a job, find a wife, buy a house with a thoroughly modern kitchen with a dog called Carlos with some kind of strange skin disease. That life did not appeal to him in the least, much to the annoyance of his girl friend. <br /><br />In part this is a book about opposites attracting (a theme Maugham is quite fond of), and it would be hard to find two people quite as opposite as this young man and the woman who spends the rest of her life loving him – in her own special way. Which is the other thing Maugham is particularly good at: the absurdity, if not utter bloody tragedy, of love. <br /><br />Now, this book is also about Western fascination with Eastern mysticism. I feel I’m at a bit of a cross-roads in life at the moment, the kids are an age when they will be soon gone and I’m losing interest in the work I do – so I had thought that perhaps reading a book about someone who chose to live the life of the spirit, to avoid popular conventions and to seek his own path would resonate with me, perhaps even to the point of shattering me. <br />And look, this book nearly did. The stuff where the girl friend was talking about the material stuff she wanted to fill her life with made me smile one of my bitter little smiles. But the problem for me was that anyone who ends up an Eastern mystic is completely lost to me. And I can’t even blame the Beatles, in this case. All of the talk about meditation and spiritual awakening and atonement with the oneness of the universe just leaves me bored out of my mind. The thought that someone could spend so much time and effort searching only to find so little at the end of that journey is infinitely depressing to me.<br /><br />So, I was a little annoyed with where this journey ended up, but like life itself, it is the journey and not the destination that is of interest, and this book is a pleasant enough journey. Better than that, even.<br /><br />Some of my Good Reads friends have said in their reviews that this was a better book than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31548.Of_Human_Bondage" title="Of Human Bondage by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Of Human Bondage</a> – I didn’t find that to be the case. But perhaps because I found Bondage to be so much more a personal book, a book that only the author could have written and one that clearly cost the author much more to write than this one could ever have cost him. It will probably always remain one of my favourite novels. But this one was good, and well worth the time.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1862437818475288170" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/792161" />
    <a title="A Rye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52891-a-rye"><img alt="A Rye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351130633p2/52891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/792161?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="A Rye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="A Rye" href="/user/show/52891-a-rye">A Rye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those who used to identify with Holden Caulfield, but are growing up.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52891-a-rye?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer792161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14685019282415958525">Boring for most, enlightening for some, and absolutely beautiful to very few, this book describes the journey of a man disillusioned with the materialistic predilections of society. <br/>After some rather traumatic experiences as a fighter pilot in WW I, American Larry Darrell relocates to France, where he dedicates himself to a life of learning and seeking. A pilgrimage to India results in a spiritual revelation for Darrell, and it is at this point that his entire world begins to shift. <br/>Stylisticall</span>
  <span id="freeText14685019282415958525" style="display:none">Boring for most, enlightening for some, and absolutely beautiful to very few, this book describes the journey of a man disillusioned with the materialistic predilections of society. <br />After some rather traumatic experiences as a fighter pilot in WW I, American Larry Darrell relocates to France, where he dedicates himself to a life of learning and seeking. A pilgrimage to India results in a spiritual revelation for Darrell, and it is at this point that his entire world begins to shift. <br />Stylistically, Maugham employs a very innovative approach - rather than focusing on the changes that occur directly in Darrell's life, the narrative follows how others react to Larry's continual tranformation.<br /><br />If you don't wish to spend the time reading this, the 1984 film version (starring Bill Murray in one of his first dramatic roles) is fantastic, and very faithful to the novel. In fact, this may be one of the rare exceptions where a film actually does a better job of capturing the story.<br /><br /><br />"The sharp edge of a razor is difficult to pass over; thus the wise say the path to Salvation is hard."</span>
  <a data-text-id="14685019282415958525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51089-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1286573170p2/51089.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/732612535?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/51089-jamie">Jamie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51089-jamie?shelf=the-classics">the-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer732612535" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5134994600852353726">Maugham, you son of a bitch.<br/><br/><i>“You know, at one time I made quite a little reputation for myself as a humorist by the simple process of telling the truth. It came as such a surprise to most people that they thought I was being funny.”</i><br/><br/>There are the first thirty pages of a Somerset Maugham book, and then there are the next 200, or 300, or 400 pages that turn it on its head. It’s a game now for me, to form as fast and as sure an opinion of the people he puts in those first thirty pages so the rest of</span>
  <span id="freeText5134994600852353726" style="display:none">Maugham, you son of a bitch.<br /><br /><i>“You know, at one time I made quite a little reputation for myself as a humorist by the simple process of telling the truth. It came as such a surprise to most people that they thought I was being funny.”</i><br /><br />There are the first thirty pages of a Somerset Maugham book, and then there are the next 200, or 300, or 400 pages that turn it on its head. It’s a game now for me, to form as fast and as sure an opinion of the people he puts in those first thirty pages so the rest of the book can tear it all to shreds. They’re wonderful, one and all. They’re petty, they’re profound, they’re stubborn and righteous and vulnerable and kind and cruel until they’re just stubbornly and profoundly human, defying categorization, defying us to cast the first stone. <br /><br />Each one is on their own journey. Each one is nowhere near the end. And once again, I’m agreeing with the umbrage Maugham takes (in this paragraph, via Larry) with a God that lacks the common sense to see through all of it:<br /><br /><blockquote>“I’d known a lot of fellows in the air corps. Of course they got drunk when they got a chance, and had a girl whenever they could, and used foul language; we had one or two bad hats: one fellow was arrested for passing rubber checks and was sent to prison for six months; it wasn’t altogether his fault; he’d never had any money before, and when he got more than he’d ever dreamt of having, it went to his head. I’d known bad men in Paris, and when I got back to Chicago I knew more, but for the most part their badness was due to heredity, which they couldn’t help, or to their environment, which they didn’t choose: I’m not sure that society wasn’t more responsible for their crimes than they were. If I’d been God I couldn’t have brought myself to condemn one of them, not even the worst, to eternal damnation.”</blockquote><br /><br />If I say, in the end, it wasn’t quite <i>Of Human Bondage</i> for me, or <i>The Moon and Sixpence</i>, or <i>The Painted Veil</i>, that’s like saying this one rich meal was less rich than the others just because it wasn’t the first. That’s no way to judge it. Thankfully, Maugham puts himself in the book— more an autobiography in parts than a novel— so that I can use his words about Isabel to describe how I feel, always, about Maugham himself:<br /><br /><i>“I liked her for saying that. One of her most amiable traits was that she was never affronted by the naked truth.”</i><br /><br />And I think he’d like me for saying this: Maugham, you son of a bitch.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5134994600852353726" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15514196423716073729">It took me a long time to read this book, this beautiful book, this excellent book. I took time, because every sentence, every word deserves to be read carefully. It indeed served by subtle prose, sought after in its simplicity.<br/>The stories are full.   <span id="freeText15514196423716073729" style="display:none">It took me a long time to read this book, this beautiful book, this excellent book. I took time, because every sentence, every word deserves to be read carefully. It indeed served by subtle prose, sought after in its simplicity.<br />The stories are full. They demonstrate how each life carries a greater or lesser share of tragedy and ridicule; how happiness cannot be an exact science, it is even happy that each one has its definition: it can hide in futility as in the Absolute. They also allow you to position yourself facing each of the characters described in this beautiful book, this lovely book.</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bA1ewhaTOLDEyUfrOKZ87Szykstci7nim8xKdtqzbipWKzcQrc3x2GPi84rPU3Yy+q11A5IGN4XAsgzHF/CcsA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('V24c9PSmJOYgxnv5nsXXfWey8hJcAKSeHKUxomf5cyZtSHUmT/jtjoftz5hpMN2ise0V2pKNKvlH23cTqrqBvA==')})
  }

Somerset Maugham’s own true story has never been fully told. At last, the fascinating truth is revealed in a landmark biography by the award-winning writer Selina Hastings. Granted unprecedented access to Maugham’s personal correspondence and to newly uncovered interviews with his only child, Hastings portrays the secret loves, betrayals, integrity, and passion that inspired Maugham to create such classics as The Razor’s Edge and Of Human Bondage.Hastings vividly presents Maugham’s lonely childhood spent with unloving relatives after the death of his parents, a trauma that resulted in shyness, a stammer, and for the rest of his life an urgent need for physical tenderness. Here, too, are his adult triumphs on the stage and page, works that allowed him a glittering social life in which he befriended and sometimes fell out with such luminaries as Dorothy Parker, Charlie Chaplin, D. H. Lawrence, and Winston Churchill.The Secret Lives of Somerset Maugham portrays in full for the first time Maugham’s disastrous marriage to Syrie Wellcome, a manipulative society woman of dubious morality who trapped Maugham with a pregnancy and an attempted suicide. Hastings also explores Maugham’s many affairs with men, including his great love, Gerald Haxton, an alcoholic charmer and a cad. Maugham’s courageous work in secret intelligence during two world wars is described in fascinating detail—experiences that provided the inspiration for the groundbreaking Ashenden stories. From the West End to Broadway, from China to the South Pacific, Maugham’s restless and remarkably productive life is thrillingly recounted as Hastings uncovers the real stories behind such classics as “Rain,” The Painted Veil, Cakes &amp; Ale, and other well-known tales.An epic biography of a hugely talented and hugely conflicted man, The Secret Lives of Somerset Maugham is the definitive account Durante a Segunda Guerra Mundial, um convento francês que albergava treze pessoas poderia evitar ser bombardeado pelos alemães, pintando o nome «Missão» no telhado. Assim, distinguir-se-ia de uma fábrica com arquitectura igual, onde trabalhavam quatro centenas de operários, que constituía um alvo militar prioritário. Porém, desse modo, a localização da fábrica pelos aviões inimigos tornar-se-ia mais fácil. A novela descreve a discussão entre o Superior, que advogava a identificação da Missão, e o frade Georges Mounier, que a contestava. Todos os argumentos – desde o costume internacional até à vontade de Deus, passando pelo número de vidas em perigo – foram esgrimidos. Abalado, o Superior promoveu uma reunião dos religiosos, que se revelou inconclusiva, e pediu instruções superiores. Entretanto, a França capitulou e o mosteiro foi ocupado pelos alemães, que decidiram, de imediato, fazer a pintura do telhado para prevenir um bombardeamento inglês.\n\nFernanda Palma, 18 Julho WATT was the beginning of Samuel Becket\'s post-war literary career, the fruition of the years in hiding in the Vaucluse mountains from the Gestapo, which also largely inspired WAITING FOR GODOT. But it remains, unlike the work that followed it, extremely Irish, a philosophical novel full of the grim humour that was already his trade-mark in such earlier fictions as MORE PRICKS THAN KICKS and MURPHY. The perambulations of WATT, especially in the home of the eccentric Mr. Knott, and the sketching of logic to elicit meaning, must be among the most comic inventions of modern literature. First published by the libertine Olympia Press in 1953, it has established itself as one of the most quoted and best-loved of Becket\'s novels. Antonio Muñoz Molina transmite como nadie la fragilidad de instantes capaces de cambiar una vida, como la llegada del primer televisor a casa, la conciencia del incalculable consuelo de la lectura o el descubrimiento de un secreto que ha marcado a la ciudad desde la guerra civil.\n \nHistoria de iniciación magistralmente narrada, El viento de la Luna posee elementos que remiten al mundo de escritores como Salinger o Philip Roth, pero también es un nuevo episodio en el ciclo narrativo de Mágina, como reconocerán enseguida los lectores de Beatus Ille y El jinete polaco. Set in fin-de-siècle Austria—the cafés, salons, and musical concerts frequented by the Viennese elite—Schnitzler\'s perceptive exploration of the creative process and the private lives and public aspirations of urban Jewish intellectuals ranks with the highest achievements of Karl Kraus and Robert Musil.The novel\'s central character, Baron Georg von Wergenthin, is a handsome young composer whose troubled relations with women, musical collaborators, and representatives of the old social order make Schnitzler\'s book a revealing investigation of individual psychology and social allegory. Somerset Maugham</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10152631859530403658">William Somerset Maugham was born in Paris in 1874. He spoke French even before he spoke a word of English, a fact to which some critics attribute the purity of his style. <br/><br/>His parents died early and, after an unhappy boyhood, which he recorded poignantly in <i>Of Human Bondage</i>, Maugham became a qualified physician. But writing was his true vocation. For ten years before his first success, he almost l</span>
  <span id="freeText10152631859530403658" style="display:none">William Somerset Maugham was born in Paris in 1874. He spoke French even before he spoke a word of English, a fact to which some critics attribute the purity of his style. <br /><br />His parents died early and, after an unhappy boyhood, which he recorded poignantly in <i>Of Human Bondage</i>, Maugham became a qualified physician. But writing was his true vocation. For ten years before his first success, he almost literally starved while pouring out novels and plays. <br /><br />Maugham wrote at a time when experimental modernist literature such as that of William Faulkner, Thomas Mann, James Joyce and Virginia Woolf was gaining increasing popularity and winning critical acclaim. In this context, his plain prose style was criticized as 'such a tissue of clichés' that one's wonder is finally aroused at the writer's ability to assemble so many and at his unfailing inability to put anything in an individual way.<br /><br />During World War I, Maugham worked for the British Secret Service . He travelled all over the world, and made many visits to America. After World War II, Maugham made his home in south of France and continued to move between England and Nice till his death in 1965.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10152631859530403658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
